     <p>
      <b>
       Thales of Miletus
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// 'th' in 'thigh'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="/e/ long 'a' in 'base'">
           e
          </span>
          <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
           l
          </span>
          <span title="/i/ long 'e' in 'bead'">
           i
          </span>
          <span title="'z' in 'Zion'">
           z
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
       <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%98%CE%B1%CE%BB%E1%BF%86%CF%82" title="wikt:">
        
       </a>
       ( )
      </span>
      ,
      <i>
       Thals
      </i>
      ;
      <abbr title="circa">
       c.
      </abbr>
      624 c. 546BC) was a
      <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">
       pre-Socratic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">
       Greek philosopher
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Mathematics" title="Greek Mathematics">
       mathematician
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">
       Miletus
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
       Asia Minor
      </a>
      and one of the
      <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sages_of_Greece" title="Seven Sages of Greece">
       Seven Sages of Greece
      </a>
      . Many, most notably
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      , regard him as the first philosopher in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">
       Greek tradition
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPM_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CPM-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aristotle reported Thales'
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">
       hypothesis
      </a>
      that the
      <a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">
       originating principle
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">
       nature
      </a>
      and the nature of
      <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">
       matter
      </a>
      was a single material
      <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">
       substance
      </a>
      :
      <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">
       water
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales attempted to explain natural phenomena without reference to
      <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">
       mythology
      </a>
      . Almost all of the other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic" title="Pre-Socratic">
       Pre-Socratic
      </a>
      philosophers follow him in attempting to provide an explanation of ultimate substance, change, and the existence of the world without reference to mythology.
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">
       mathematics
      </a>
      , Thales used
      <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">
       geometry
      </a>
      to calculate the heights of pyramids and the distance of ships from the shore. He is the first known individual to use
      <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">
       deductive reasoning
      </a>
      applied to geometry, by deriving four corollaries to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales%27_Theorem" title="Thales' Theorem">
       Thales' Theorem
      </a>
      . He is the first known individual to whom a mathematical discovery has been attributed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14">
        <a href="#Interpretations">

         <span class="toctext">
          Interpretations
         </span>
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15">
          <a href="#Theory">

           </span>

           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16">

           <span class="tocnumber">

           </span>

            Rise of theoretical inquiry
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17">

           </span>

            Classification
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">
        <a href="#Influence_on_others">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          6
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Influence on others
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">

         <span class="tocnumber">

         </span>
         <span class="toctext">

         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20">

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">

     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life">
       Life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The current historical consensus is that Thales was born in the city of
      <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">
       Miletus
      </a>
      around the mid 620s BC. Miletus was an ancient
      <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">
       Greek Ionian
      </a>
      city on the western coast of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
       Asia Minor
      </a>
      (in what is today
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aydin_Province" title="Aydin Province">
       Aydin Province
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
       Turkey
      </a>
      ), near the mouth of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maeander_River" title="Maeander River">
       Maeander River
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The dates of Thales' life are not exactly known but are roughly established by a few datable events mentioned in the sources. According to
      <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
       Herodotus
      </a>
      (and as determined by modern methods), Thales predicted the
      <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_of_Thales" title="Eclipse of Thales">
       solar eclipse of May 28, 585BC
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Lartius">
       Diogenes Lartius
      </a>
      quotes the chronicle of
      <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_of_Athens" title="Apollodorus of Athens">
       Apollodorus of Athens
      </a>
      as saying that Thales died at the age of 78 during the 58th
      <a href="/wiki/Olympiad" title="Olympiad">
       Olympiad
      </a>
      (548545BC) and attributes his death to heat stroke while watching the games.
     </p>
     <p>
      Diogenes Lartius states that ("according to Herodotus and Douris and
      <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">
       Democritus
      </a>
      ") Thales' parents were Examyes and Cleobuline, then traces the family line back to
      <a href="/wiki/Cadmus" title="Cadmus">
       Cadmus
      </a>
      , a mythological
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenician
      </a>
      prince of
      <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">
       Tyre
      </a>
      . Diogenes then delivers conflicting reports: one that Thales married and either fathered a son (
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cybisthus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cybisthus (page does not exist)">
       Cybisthus
      </a>
      or
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cybisthon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cybisthon (page does not exist)">
       Cybisthon
      </a>
      ) or adopted his nephew of the same name; the second that he never married, telling his mother as a young man that it was too early to marry, and as an older man that it was too late.
      <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
       Plutarch
      </a>
      had earlier told this version:
      <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">
       Solon
      </a>
      visited Thales and asked him why he remained single; Thales answered that he did not like the idea of having to worry about children. Nevertheless, several years later, anxious for family, he adopted his nephew Cybisthus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales involved himself in many activities, taking the role of an innovator. Some say that he left no writings, others say that he wrote
      <i>
       On the Solstice
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       On the Equinox
      </i>
      . (No writing attributed to him has survived.) Diogenes Lartius quotes two letters from Thales: one to
      <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Syros" title="Pherecydes of Syros">
       Pherecydes of Syros
      </a>
      offering to review his book on religion, and one to
      <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">
       Solon
      </a>
      , offering to keep him company on his sojourn from
      <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
       Athens
      </a>
      . Thales identifies the Milesians as Athenian
      <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity#Greek_colonies" title="Colonies in antiquity">
       colonists
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Business">
       Business
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Business">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/330px-Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/440px-Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Capernaum_roman_olive_press_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An olive mill and an olive press dating from Roman times in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capernaum,_Israel" title="Capernaum, Israel">
         Capernaum, Israel
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Several anecdotes suggest that Thales was not solely a thinker but was also involved in business and politics. One story recounts that he bought all the
      <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_extraction#Traditional_method:_olive_press" title="Olive oil extraction">
       olive presses
      </a>
      in Miletus after predicting the weather and a good harvest for a particular year. In another version of the same story, Aristotle explains that Thales reserved presses ahead of time at a discount only to rent them out at a high price when demand peaked, following his predictions of a particularly good harvest. This first version of the story would constitute the first creation and use of
      <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">
       futures
      </a>
      , whereas the second version would be the first creation and use of
      <a href="/wiki/Option_(finance)" title="Option (finance)">
       options
      </a>
      . Aristotle explains that Thales' objective in doing this was not to enrich himself but to prove to his fellow Milesians that philosophy could be useful, contrary to what they thought.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
       Politics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Politics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Thales political life had mainly to do with the involvement of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">
       Ionians
      </a>
      in the defense of
      <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
       Anatolia
      </a>
      against the growing power of the
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Persians
      </a>
      , who were then new to the region. A king had come to power in neighboring
      <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
       Lydia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">
       Croesus
      </a>
      , who was somewhat too aggressive for the size of his army. He had conquered most of the states of coastal Anatolia, including the cities of the Ionians. The story is told in
      <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
       Herodotus
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Lydians were at war with the
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      , a remnant of the first wave of Iranians in the region, over the issue of refuge the Lydians had given to some
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scythian" title="Scythian">
       Scythian
      </a>
      soldiers of fortune inimical to the Medes. The war endured for five years, but in the sixth an eclipse of the Sun (mentioned above) spontaneously halted a battle in progress (the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Halys_(585_BC)" title="Battle of Halys (585 BC)">
       Battle of Halys
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3489" data-file-width="3543" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/220px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/330px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg/440px-Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1999_4_NR.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Total
        <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">
         eclipse
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      It seems that Thales had predicted this
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
       solar eclipse
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sages_of_Greece" title="Seven Sages of Greece">
       Seven Sages
      </a>
      were most likely already in existence, as Croesus was also heavily influenced by
      <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">
       Solon
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
       Athens
      </a>
      , another sage. Whether Thales was present at the battle is not known, nor are the exact terms of the prediction, but based on it the Lydians and Medes made peace immediately, swearing a blood oath.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Medes were dependencies of the
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Persians
      </a>
      under
      <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
       Cyrus
      </a>
      . Croesus now sided with the Medes against the Persians and marched in the direction of Iran (with far fewer men than he needed). He was stopped by the river
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halys_River" title="Halys River">
       Halys
      </a>
      , then unbridged. This time he had Thales with him, perhaps by invitation. Whatever his status, the king gave the problem to him, and he got the army across by digging a diversion upstream so as to reduce the flow, making it possible to ford the river. The channels ran around both sides of the camp.
     </p>
     <p>
      The two armies engaged at
      <a href="/wiki/Pteria_(Cappadocia)" title="Pteria (Cappadocia)">
       Pteria
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">
       Cappadocia
      </a>
      . As the battle was indecisive but paralyzing to both sides, Croesus marched home, dismissed his mercenaries and sent emissaries to his dependents and allies to ask them to dispatch fresh troops to
      <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">
       Sardis
      </a>
      . The issue became more pressing when the Persian army showed up at Sardis.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_Laertius" title="Diogenes Laertius">
       Diogenes Laertius
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      tells us that Thales gained fame as a counselor when he advised the Milesians not to engage in a symmachia, a "fighting together", with the Lydians. This has sometimes been interpreted as an alliance, but a ruler does not ally with his subjects.
     </p>
     <p>
      Croesus was defeated before the city of Sardis by Cyrus, who subsequently spared Miletus because it had taken no action. Cyrus was so impressed by Croesus wisdom and his connection with the sages that he spared him and took his advice on various matters.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Ionians were now free. Herodotus says that Thales advised them to form an Ionian state; that is, a bouleuterion ("deliberative body") to be located at
      <a href="/wiki/Teos" title="Teos">
       Teos
      </a>
      in the center of
      <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">
       Ionia
      </a>
      . The Ionian cities should be demoi, or "districts". Miletus, however, received favorable terms from Cyrus. The others remained in an Ionian League of 12 cities (excluding Miletus now), and were subjugated by the Persians.
     </p>
     <p>
      While Herodotus reported that most of his fellow Greeks believe that Thales did divert the river Halys to assist King Croesus' military endeavors, he himself finds it doubtful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sagacity">
       Sagacity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sagacity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MiletusIonicStoa.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MiletusIonicStoa.jpg/220px-MiletusIonicStoa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MiletusIonicStoa.jpg/330px-MiletusIonicStoa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MiletusIonicStoa.jpg/440px-MiletusIonicStoa.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MiletusIonicStoa.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Ionic Stoa on the Sacred Way in Miletus
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_Laertius" title="Diogenes Laertius">
       Diogenes Laertius
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      tells us that the
      <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sages_of_Greece" title="Seven Sages of Greece">
       Seven Sages
      </a>
      were created in the archonship of Damasius at
      <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
       Athens
      </a>
      about 582BC and that Thales was the first sage. The same story, however, asserts that Thales emigrated to
      <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">
       Miletus
      </a>
      . There is also a report that he did not become a student of nature until after his political career. Much as we would like to have a date on the seven sages, we must reject these stories and the tempting date if we are to believe that Thales was a native of Miletus, predicted the eclipse, and was with
      <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">
       Croesus
      </a>
      in the campaign against
      <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
       Cyrus
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales received instruction from an
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
       Egyptian
      </a>
      priest. It was fairly certain that he came from a wealthy, established family, in a class which customarily provided higher education for their children. Moreover, the ordinary citizen, unless he was a seafaring man or a merchant, could not afford the grand tour in
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      , and did not consort with noble lawmakers such as
      <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">
       Solon
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In Diogenes Laertius'
      <i>
       Lives of Eminent Philosophers
      </i>
      Chapter 1.39, Laertius relates the several stories of an expensive object that is to go to the most wise. In one version (that Laertius credits to
      <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">
       Callimachus
      </a>
      in his
      <i>
       Iambics
      </i>
      ) Bathycles of Arcadia states in his will that an expensive bowl "'should be given to him who had done most good by his wisdom.' So it was given to Thales, went the round of all the sages, and came back to Thales again. And he sent it to Apollo at Didyma, with this dedication...'Thales the Milesian, son of Examyas [dedicates this] to Delphinian Apollo after twice winning the prize from all the Greeks.'"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Theories">
       Theories
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Theories">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The Greeks often invoked idiosyncratic explanations of natural phenomena with reference to the will of
      <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">
       anthropomorphic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
       gods
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">
       heroes
      </a>
      . Instead, Thales aimed to explain natural phenomena via rational hypotheses that referenced natural processes themselves. For example, rather than assuming that earthquakes were the result of supernatural whims Thales explained them by hypothesizing that the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      floats on water and that earthquakes occur when the Earth is rocked by waves.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales was a
      <a href="/wiki/Hylozoism" title="Hylozoism">
       hylozoist
      </a>
      (one who thinks that matter is alive).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      That interpretation by later commentatorsthat Thales treated matter as being alivemay have been substituted for his thinking that the properties of nature arise directly from material processes. The latter thesis is more consistent with modern ideas of how properties arise as emergent characteristics of those
      <a href="/wiki/Complex_systems" title="Complex systems">
       complex systems
      </a>
      involved in the processes of
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
       evolution
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(biology)" title="Human development (biology)">
       developmental
      </a>
      change.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales, according to
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      , asked what was the nature (Greek
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">
        arche
       </a>
      </i>
      ) of the object so that it would behave in its characteristic way.
      <a href="/wiki/Physis" title="Physis">
       Physis
      </a>
      () comes from phyein (), "to grow", related to our word "be".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       (G)natura
      </i>
      is the way a thing is "born",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      again with the stamp of what it is in itself.
     </p>
     <p>
      Aristotle
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      characterizes most of the philosophers "at first" (
      <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
       
      </span>
      ) as thinking that the "principles in the form of matter were the only principles of all things", where "principle" is
      <a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">
       arche
      </a>
      , "matter" is
      <a href="/wiki/Hyle" title="Hyle">
       hyle
      </a>
      ("wood" or "matter", "material") and "form" is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eidos_(philosophy)" title="Eidos (philosophy)">
       eidos
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Arche
      </i>
      is translated as "principle", but the two words do not have precisely the same meaning. A
      <a href="/wiki/Principle" title="Principle">
       principle
      </a>
      of something is merely prior (related to pro-) to it either chronologically or logically. An arche (from
      <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
       
      </span>
      , "to rule") dominates an object in some way. If the arche is taken to be an origin, then specific causality is implied; that is, B is supposed to be characteristically B just because it comes from A, which dominates it.
     </p>
     <p>
      The archai that Aristotle had in mind in his well-known passage on the first Greek scientists are not necessarily chronologically prior to their objects, but are constituents of it. For example, in pluralism objects are composed of earth, air, fire and water, but those elements do not disappear with the production of the object. They remain as archai within it, as do the atoms of the atomists.
     </p>
     <p>
      What Aristotle is really saying is that the first philosophers were trying to define the substance(s) of which all material objects are composed. As a matter of fact, that is exactly what modern scientists are attempting to accomplish in nuclear physics, which is a second reason why Thales is described as the first western scientist.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry">
       Geometry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geometry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Thales was known for his innovative use of
      <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">
       geometry
      </a>
      . His understanding was theoretical as well as practical. For example, he said:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Megiston topos: hapanta gar chorei (    )
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Space is the greatest thing, as it contains all things
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Topos is in Newtonian-style
      <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">
       space
      </a>
      , since the verb, chorei, has the connotation of yielding before things, or spreading out to make room for them, which is
      <a href="/wiki/Extension_(metaphysics)" title="Extension (metaphysics)">
       extension
      </a>
      . Within this extension, things have a position.
      <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">
       Points
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Line_(mathematics)" title="Line (mathematics)">
       lines
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_(mathematics)" title="Plane (mathematics)">
       planes
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
       solids
      </a>
      related by
      <a href="/wiki/Distance" title="Distance">
       distances
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">
       angles
      </a>
      follow from this presumption.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thales understood
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Similar_triangles" title="Similar triangles">
       similar triangles
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Right_triangle" title="Right triangle">
       right triangles
      </a>
      , and what is more, used that knowledge in practical ways. The story is told in DL (loc. cit.) that he measured the height of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyramids" title="Pyramids">
       pyramids
      </a>
      by their shadows at the moment when his own shadow was equal to his height. A right triangle with two equal legs is a 45-degree right triangle, all of which are similar. The length of the pyramids shadow measured from the center of the pyramid at that moment must have been equal to its height.
     </p>
     <p>
      This story indicates that he was familiar with the Egyptian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Seked" title="Seked">
        seked
       </a>
      </i>
      , or
      <i>
       seqed
      </i>
      - the ratio of the run to the rise of a
      <a href="/wiki/Slope" title="Slope">
       slope
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotangent" title="Cotangent">
       cotangent
      </a>
      ). The
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      is at the base of problems 56, 57, 58, 59 and 60 of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhind_papyrus" title="Rhind papyrus">
       Rhind papyrus
      </a>
       an ancient Egyptian mathematical document.
     </p>
     <p>
      In present day trigonometry, cotangents require the same units for run and rise (base and perpendicular), but the papyrus uses
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubits" title="Cubits">
       cubits
      </a>
      for rise and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palm_(measurement)" title="Palm (measurement)">
       palms
      </a>
      for run, resulting in different (but still characteristic) numbers. Since there were 7 palms in a cubit, the
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      was 7 times the cotangent.
     </p>
     <p>
      To use an example often quoted in modern reference works, suppose the base of a pyramid is 140cubits and the angle of rise 5.25
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      . The Egyptians expressed their fractions as the sum of fractions, but the decimals are sufficient for the example. What is the rise in cubits? The run is 70cubits, 490 palms. X, the rise, is 490 divided by 5.25 or 93
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
      </span>
      cubits. These figures sufficed for the Egyptians and Thales. We would go on to calculate the cotangent as 70 divided by 93
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
      </span>
      to get 3/4 or .75 and looking that up in a table of cotangents find that the angle of rise is a few minutes over 53degrees.
     </p>
     <p>
      Whether the ability to use the
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      , which preceded Thales by about 1000years, means that he was the first to define trigonometry is a matter of opinion. More practically Thales used the same method to measure the distances of ships at sea, said Eudemus as reported by
      <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">
       Proclus
      </a>
      (in Euclidem). According to Kirk &amp; Raven (reference cited below), all you need for this feat is three straight sticks pinned at one end and knowledge of your altitude. One stick goes vertically into the ground. A second is made level. With the third you sight the ship and calculate the
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      from the height of the stick and its distance from the point of insertion to the line of sight.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      is a measure of the angle. Knowledge of two angles (the
      <i>
       seked
      </i>
      and a right angle) and an enclosed leg (the altitude) allows you to determine by similar triangles the second leg, which is the distance. Thales probably had his own equipment rigged and recorded his own
      <i>
       sekeds
      </i>
      , but that is only a guess.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thales.E2.80.99_Theorem">
       Thales Theorem
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Thales Theorem">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thales_theorem_1.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="583" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Thales_theorem_1.png/300px-Thales_theorem_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Thales_theorem_1.png/450px-Thales_theorem_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d5/Thales_theorem_1.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thales_theorem_1.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Thales%27_theorem" title="Thales' theorem">
         Thales' Theorem
        </a>
        :
        <img alt="\textstyle \frac{DE}{BC} = \frac{AE}{AC } = \frac{AD}{AB}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/a/9/9/a9984b730b4ec71f978bec17db833a7e.png"/>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales%E2%80%99_theorem" title="Thales theorem">
       Thales theorem
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">
       Pythagorean theorem
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Actually there are two theorems called Theorem of Thales, one having to do with a triangle inscribed in a circle and having the circle's diameter as one leg, the other theorem being also called the
      <a href="/wiki/Intercept_theorem" title="Intercept theorem">
       intercept theorem
      </a>
      . In addition
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Eudemus" title="Eudemus">
       Eudemus
      </a>
      attributed to him the discovery that a circle is
      <a href="/wiki/Bisection" title="Bisection">
       bisected
      </a>
      by its diameter, that the base angles of an isosceles triangle are equal and that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_angles" title="Vertical angles">
       vertical angles
      </a>
      are equal. According to a historical Note,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      when Thales visited Egypt, he observed that whenever the Egyptians drew two intersecting lines, they would measure the vertical angles to make sure that they were equal. Thales concluded that one could prove that all vertical angles are equal if one accepted some general notions such as: all straight angles are equal, equals added to equals are equal, and equals subtracted from equals are equal. It would be hard to imagine civilization without these theorems.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_as_a_first_principle">
       Water as a first principle
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Water as a first principle">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Thales' most famous philosophical position was his
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">
       cosmological
      </a>
      thesis, which comes down to us through a passage from
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
        Metaphysics
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the work Aristotle unequivocally reported Thales hypothesis about the nature of matter  that the originating principle of nature was
      <a href="/wiki/Material_monism" title="Material monism">
       a single material substance
      </a>
      : water. Aristotle then proceeded to proffer a number of conjectures based on his own observations to lend some credence to why Thales may have advanced this idea (though Aristotle didnt hold it himself).
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      considered Thales position to be roughly the equivalent to the later ideas of
      <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">
       Anaximenes
      </a>
      , who held that everything was composed of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">
       air
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Aristotle laid out his own thinking about
      <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">
       matter and form
      </a>
      which may shed some light on the ideas of Thales, in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
        Metaphysics
       </a>
      </i>
      983 b6 811, 1721. (The passage contains words that were later adopted by science with quite different meanings.)
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "That from which is everything that exists and from which it first becomes and into which it is rendered at last, its substance remaining under it, but transforming in qualities, that they say is the element and principle of things that are. For it is necessary that there be some nature (), either one or more than one, from which become the other things of the object being saved... Thales the founder of this type of philosophy says that it is water."
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In this quote we see Aristotle's depiction of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Problem_of_change" title="Problem of change">
       problem of change
      </a>
      and the definition of
      <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">
       substance
      </a>
      . He asked if an object changes, is it the same or different? In either case how can there be a change from one to the other? The answer is that the substance "is saved", but acquires or loses different qualities (, the things you "experience").
     </p>
     <p>
      Aristotle conjectured that Thales reached his conclusion by contemplating that the "nourishment of all things is moist and that even the hot is created from the wet and lives by it." While Aristotles conjecture on why Thales held water was the originating principle of water is his own thinking, his statement that Thales held it was water is generally accepted as genuinely originating with Thales and he is seen as an incipient matter-and-formist.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus_(commentator)" title="Heraclitus (commentator)">
       Heraclitus Homericus
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      states that Thales drew his conclusion from seeing moist substance turn into air, slime and earth. It seems likely that Thales viewed the Earth as solidifying from the water on which it floated and the oceans that surround it.
     </p>
     <p>
      Writing centuries later, Diogenes Laertius also states that Thales taught "Water constituted (
      <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
       
      </span>
      , 'stood under') the principle of all things."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The 1870 book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Greek_and_Roman_Biography_and_Mythology" title="Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology">
        Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology
       </a>
      </i>
      noted
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPM_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CPM-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Thales dogma that water is the origin of things, that is, that it is that out of which every thing arises, and into which every thing resolves itself, Thales may have followed
       <a href="/wiki/Orphism_(religion)" title="Orphism (religion)">
        Orphic
       </a>
       cosmogonies, while, unlike them, he sought to establish the truth of the assertion. Hence, Aristotle, immediately after he has called him the originator of philosophy brings forward the reasons which Thales was believed to have adduced in confirmation of that assertion; for that no written development of it, or indeed any book by
       <i>
        Thales
       </i>
       , was extant, is proved by the expressions which Aristotle uses when he brings forward the doctrines and proofs of the
       <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">
        Milesian
       </a>
       . (p. 1016)
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">
       Influences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Influences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Later
      <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">
       scholastic
      </a>
      thinkers would maintain that in his choice of water Thales was influenced by Babylonian or Chaldean religion, that held that a god had begun creation by acting upon the pre-existing water. Historian Abraham Feldman holds this does not stand up under closer examination. In Babylonian religion the water is lifeless and sterile until a god acts upon it, but for Thales water itself was divine and creative. He maintained that "All things are full of gods", and to understand the nature of things was to discover the secrets of the deities, and through this knowledge open the possibility that one could be greater than the grandest Olympian.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Feldman points out that while other thinkers recognized the wetness of the world "none of them was inspired to conclude that everything was ultimately aquatic."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He further points out that Thales was "a wealthy citizen of the fabulously rich Oriental port of Miletus...a dealer in the staples of antiquity, wine and oil...He certainly handled the shell-fish of the Phoenicians that secreted the dye of imperial purple."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Feldman recalls the stories of Thales measuring the distance of boats in the harbor, creating mechanical improvements for ship navigation, giving an explanation for the flooding of the Nile (vital to Egyptian agriculture and Greek trade), and changing the course of the river Halys so an army could ford it. Rather than seeing water as a barrier Thales contemplated the Ionian yearly religious gathering for athletic ritual (held on the promontory of Mycale and believed to be ordained by the ancestral kindred of Poseidon, the god of the sea). He called for the Ionian mercantile states participating in this ritual to convert it into a democratic federation under the protection of Poseidon that would hold off the forces of pastoral Persia. Feldman concludes that Thales saw "that water was a revolutionary leveler and the elemental factor determining the subsistence and business of the world"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and "the common channel of states."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Feldman considers Thales' environment and holds that Thales would have seen tears, sweat, and blood as granting value to a person's work and the means how life giving commodities travelled (whether on bodies of water or through the sweat of slaves and pack-animals). He would have seen that minerals could be processed from water such as life-sustaining salt and gold taken from rivers. He wouldve seen fish and other food stuffs gathered from it. Feldman points out that Thales held that the lodestone was alive as it drew metals to itself. He holds that Thales "living ever in sight of his beloved sea" would see water seem to draw all "traffic in wine and oil, milk and honey, juices and dyes" to itself, leading him to "a vision of the universe melting into a single substance that was valueless in itself and still the source of wealth."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Feldman concludes that for Thales "...water united all things. The social significance of water in the time of Thales induced him to discern through hardware and dry-goods, through soil and sperm, blood, sweat and tears, one fundamental fluid stuff...water, the most commonplace and powerful material known to him."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This combined with his contemporarys idea of "
      <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">
       spontaneous generation
      </a>
      " allow us to see how Thales could hold that water could be divine and creative.
     </p>
     <p>
      Feldman points to the lasting association of the theory that "all whatness is wetness" with Thales himself, pointing out that Diogenes Laertius speaks of a poem, probably a satire, where Thales is snatched to heaven by the sun, "Perhaps it was an elaborate paronomasia based on the fact that
      <i>
       thal
      </i>
      was the Phoenician word for dew."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_21-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Feldman-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_in_divinity">
       Beliefs in divinity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Beliefs in divinity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Neopythagoreanism" title="Neopythagoreanism">
       Neopythagoreanism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">
       Neoplatonism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thales applied his method to objects that changed to become other objects, such as water into earth (or so he thought). But what about the changing itself? Thales did address the topic, approaching it through
      <a href="/wiki/Lodestone" title="Lodestone">
       lodestone
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">
       amber
      </a>
      , which, when electrified by rubbing together, also attracts. It is noteworthy that the first particle known to carry
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
       electric charge
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electron
      </a>
      , is named for the Greek word for amber,  (
      <i>
       lektron
      </i>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      How was the power to move other things without the movers changing to be explained? Thales saw a commonality with the powers of living things to act. The lodestone and the amber must be alive, and if that were so, there could be no difference between the living and the dead. When asked why he didnt die if there was no difference, he replied because there is no difference.
     </p>
     <p>
      Aristotle defined the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">
       soul
      </a>
      as the principle of life, that which imbues the matter and makes it live, giving it the animation, or power to act. The idea did not originate with him, as the Greeks in general believed in the distinction between mind and matter, which was ultimately to lead to a distinction not only between body and soul but also between matter and energy.
     </p>
     <p>
      If things were alive, they must have souls. This belief was no innovation, as the ordinary ancient populations of the Mediterranean did believe that natural actions were caused by divinities. Accordingly, the sources
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2014)">
          which?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      say that Thales believed that "all things were full of gods."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In their zeal to make him the first in everything some said he was the first to hold the belief, which must have been widely known to be false.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, Thales was looking for something more general, a universal substance of mind.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      That also was in the polytheism of the times.
      <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
       Zeus
      </a>
      was the very personification of supreme
      <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">
       mind
      </a>
      , dominating all the subordinate manifestations. From Thales on, however, philosophers had a tendency to depersonify or objectify mind, as though it were the substance of animation per se and not actually a god like the other gods. The end result was a total removal of mind from substance, opening the door to a non-divine principle of action.
     </p>
     <p>
      Classical thought, however, had proceeded only a little way along that path. Instead of referring to the person, Zeus, they talked about the great mind:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "Thales", says
       <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">
        Cicero
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         23
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       "assures that
       <i>
        water
       </i>
       is the principle of all things; and that God is that Mind which shaped and created all things from water."
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The universal mind appears as a Roman belief in
      <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">
       Virgil
      </a>
      as well:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <i>
        "In the beginning, SPIRIT within (spiritus intus) strengthens Heaven and Earth
       </i>
       ,
       <br/>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       <i>
        The watery fields, and the lucid globe of Luna, and then --
        <br/>
       </i>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       <i>
        Titan stars; and mind (mens) infused through the limbs
       </i>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       <i>
        Agitates the whole mass, and mixes itself with GREAT MATTER (magno corpore)"
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         24
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      According to
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">
       Henry Fielding
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Lartius">
       Diogenes Lartius
      </a>
      affirmed that Thales posed "the independent pre-existence of God from all eternity, stating "that God was the oldest of all beings, for he existed without a previous cause even in the way of generation; that the world was the most beautiful of all things; for it was created by God."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation">
       Reputation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reputation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Thales (who died around 30years before the time of
      <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">
       Pythagoras
      </a>
      and 300years before
      <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">
       Euclid
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">
       Eudoxus of Cnidus
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Eudemus_of_Rhodes" title="Eudemus of Rhodes">
       Eudemus of Rhodes
      </a>
      ) is often hailed as "the first Greek mathematician".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While some historians, such as Colin R. Fletcher, point out that there could have been a predecessor to Thales who would've been named in Eudemus' lost book
      <i>
       History of Geometry
      </i>
      it is admitted that without the work "the question becomes mere speculation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Fletcher holds that as there is no viable predecessor to the title of first Greek mathematician, the only question is whether Thales qualifies as a practitioner in that field; he holds that "Thales had at his command the techniques of observation, experimentation, superposition and deductionhe has proved himself mathematician."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The evidence for the primacy of Thales comes to us from a book by
      <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">
       Proclus
      </a>
      who wrote a thousand years after Thales but is believed to have had a copy of Eudemus' book. Proclus wrote "Thales was the first to go to Egypt and bring back to Greece this study."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He goes on to tell us that in addition to applying the knowledge he gained in Egypt "He himself discovered many propositions and disclosed the underlying principles of many others to his successors, in some case his method being more general, in others more empirical."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other quotes from Proclus list more of Thales' mathematical achievements:
     </p>
     <p>
      "They say that Thales was the first to demonstrate that the circle is bisected by the diameter, the cause of the bisection being the unimpeded passage of the straight line through the centre."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      "[Thales] is said to have been the first to have known and to have enunciated [the theorem] that the angles at the base of any isosceles triangle are equal, though in the more archaic manner he described the equal angles as similar."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      "This theorem, that when two straight lines cut one another, the vertical and opposite angles are equal, was first discovered, as Eudemus says, by Thales, though the scientific demonstration was improved by the
      <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">
       writer of
       <i>
        Elements
       </i>
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      "Eudemus in his
      <i>
       History of Geometry
      </i>
      attributes this theorem [the equality of triangles having two angles and one side equal] to Thales. For he says that the method by which Thales showed how to find the distance of ships at sea necessarily involves this method."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Pamphile_of_Epidaurus" title="Pamphile of Epidaurus">
       Pamphila
      </a>
      says that, having learnt geometry from the Egyptians, he [Thales] was the first to inscribe in a circle a right-angled triangle, whereupon he
      <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">
       sacrificed an ox
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to Proclus,
      <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_of_Rhodes" title="Hieronymus of Rhodes">
       Hieronymus of Rhodes
      </a>
      also cites Thales as the first Greek mathematician. Hieronymus held that Thales was able to measure the height of the pyramids by using a
      <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">
       theorem
      </a>
      of geometry now known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Intercept_theorem#Measuring.2FSurvey" title="Intercept theorem">
       intercept theorem
      </a>
      , (after gathering data by using his walking-stick and comparing its shadow to those cast by the pyramids). We receive variations of Hieronymus' story through
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_Laertius" title="Diogenes Laertius">
       Diogenes Laertius
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
       Pliny the Elder
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
       Plutarch
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to the variations among testimonies, such as the "story of the sacrifice of an ox on the occasion of the discovery that the angle on a diameter of a circle is a right angle" in the version told by Diogenes Laertius being accredited to Pythagoras rather than Thales, some historians (such as D. R. Dicks) question whether such anecdotes have any historical worth whatsoever.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Influences_2">
       Influences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Influences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Due to the scarcity of sources concerning Thales and the diversity among the ones we possess, there is a scholarly debate over possible influences on Thales and the Greek mathematicians that came after him.
     </p>
     <p>
      Historian Roger L. Cooke points out that Proclus does not make any mention of Mesopotamian influence on Thales or Greek geometry, but "is shown clearly in Greek astronomy, in the use of sexagesimal system of measuring angles and in Ptolemy's explicit use of Mesopotamian astronomical observations."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cooke-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cooke notes that it may possibly also appear in the second book of Euclid's
      <i>
       Elements
      </i>
      , "which contains geometric constructions equivalent to certain algebraic relations that are frequently encountered in the cuneiform tablets." Cooke notes "This relation however, is controversial."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cooke-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Historian B.L. Van der Waerden is among those advocating the idea of Mesopotamian influence, writing "It follows that we have to abandon the traditional belief that the oldest Greek mathematicians discovered geometry entirely by themselvesa belief that was tenable only as long as nothing was known about Babylonian mathematics. This in no way diminishes the stature of Thales; on the contrary, his genius receives only now the honour that is due to it, the honour of having developed a logical structure for geometry, of having introduced proof into geometry."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazette_26-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-gazette-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some historians, such as D. R. Dicks takes issue with the idea that we can determine from the questionable sources we have, just how influenced Thales was by Babylonian sources. He points out that while Thales is held to have been able to calculate an eclipse using a cycle called the "Saros" held to have been "borrowed from the Babylonians", "The Babylonians, however, did not use cycles to predict solar eclipses, but computed them from observations of the latitude of the moon made shortly before the expected syzygy."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks cites historian O. Neugebauer who relates that "No Babylonian theory for predicting solar eclipse existed at 600B.C., as one can see from the very unsatisfactory situation 400year later; nor did the Babylonians ever develop any theory which took the influence of geographical latitude into account." Dicks examines the cycle referred to as 'Saros' - which Thales is held to have used and which is believed to stem from the Babylonians. He points out that Ptolemy makes use of this and another cycle in his book
      <i>
       Mathematical Syntaxis
      </i>
      but attributes it to Greek astronomers earlier than
      <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">
       Hipparchus
      </a>
      and not to Babylonians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks notes Herodotus does relate that Thales made use of a cycle to predict the eclipse, but maintains that "if so, the fulfillment of the 'prediction' was a stroke of pure luck not science".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He goes further joining with other historians (F. Martini, J.L. E. Dreyer, O. Neugebauer) in rejecting the historicity of the eclipse story altogether.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks links the story of Thales discovering the cause for a solar eclipse with Herodotus' claim that Thales discovered the cycle of the sun with relation to the solstices, and concludes "he could not possibly have possessed this knowledge which neither the Egyptians nor the Babylonians nor his immediate successors possessed."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Josephus is the only ancient historian that claims Thales visited Babylonia.
     </p>
     <p>
      Herodotus wrote that the Greeks learnt the practice of dividing the day into 12 parts, about the
      <i>
       polos
      </i>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">
       gnomon
      </a>
      from the Babylonians. (The exact meaning of his use of the word
      <i>
       polos
      </i>
      is unknown, current theories include: "the heavenly dome", "the tip of the axis of the celestial sphere", or a spherical concave sundial.) Yet even Herodotus' claims on Babylonian influence are contested by some modern historians, such as L. Zhmud, who points out that the division of the day into twelve parts (and by analogy the year) was known to the Egyptians already in the second millennium, the gnomon was known to both Egyptians and Babylonians, and the idea of the "heavenly sphere" was not used outside of Greece at this time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Less controversial than the position that Thales learnt Babylonian mathematics is the claim he was influenced by Egyptians. Pointedly historian S. N. Bychkov holds that the idea that the base angles of an isosceles triangle are equal likely came from Egypt. This is because, when building a roof for a home - having a cross section be exactly an isosceles triangle isn't crucial (as it's the ridge of the roof that must fit precisely), in contrast a symmetric square pyramid cannot have errors in the base angles of the faces or they will not fit together tightly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cooke-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Historian D.R. Dicks agrees that compared to the Greeks in the era of Thales, there was a more advanced state of mathematics among the Babylonians and especially the Egyptians - "both cultures knew the correct formulae for determining the areas and volumes of simple geometrical figures such as triangles, rectangles, trapezoids, etc.; the Egyptians could also calculate correctly the volume of the frustum of a pyramid with a square base (the Babylonians used an incorrect formula for this), and used a formula for the area of a circle...which gives a value for
      <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">
       
      </a>
      of 3.1605--a good approximation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks also agrees that this would have had an effect on Thales (whom the most ancient sources agree was interested in mathematics and astronomy) but he holds that tales of Thales' travels in these lands are pure myth.
     </p>
     <p>
      The ancient civilization and massive monuments of Egypt had "a profound and ineradicable impression on the Greeks". They attributed to Egyptians "an immemorial knowledge of certain subjects" (including geometry) and would claim Egyptian origin for some of their own ideas to try and lend them "a respectable antiquity" (such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Hermetica" title="Hermetica">
       "Hermetic" literature
      </a>
      of the Alexandrian period).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dicks holds that since Thales was a prominent figure in Greek history by the time of Eudemus but "nothing certain was known except that he lived in Miletus".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A tradition developed that as "Milesians were in a position to be able to travel widely" Thales must have gone to Egypt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Herodotus says Egypt was the birthplace of geometry he must have learnt that while there. Since he had to have been there, surely one of the theories on Nile Flooding laid out by Herodotus must have come from Thales. Likewise as he must have been in Egypt he had to have done something with the Pyramids - thus the tale of measuring them. Similar apocryphal stories exist of Pythagoras and Plato traveling to Egypt with no corroborating evidence.
     </p>
     <p>
      As the Egyptian and Babylonian geometry at the time was "essentially arithmetical", they used actual numbers and "the procedure is then described with explicit instructions as to what to do with these numbers" there was no mention of how the rules of procedure were made, and nothing toward a logically arranged corpus of generalized geometrical knowledge with analytical 'proofs' such as we find in the words of Euclid, Archimedes, and Apollonius."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
