         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      So even had Thales traveled there he could not have learnt anything about the theorems he is held to have picked up there (especially because there is no evidence that any Greeks of this age could use Egyptian hieroglyphics).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Likewise until around the second century BC and the time of Hipparchus (c. 194-120BC) the Babylonian general division of the circle into 360degrees and their sexagesimal system was unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Herodotus says almost nothing about Babylonian literature and science, and very little about their history. Some historians, like P. Schnabel, hold that the Greeks only learned more about Babylonian culture from Berossus, a Babylonian priest who is said to have set up a school in Cos around 270BC (but to what extent this had in the field of geometry is contested).
     </p>
     <p>
      Dicks points out that the primitive state of Greek mathematics and astronomical ideas exhibited by the peculiar notions of Thales' successors (such as Anaximander, Anaximenes, Xenophanes, and Heraclitus), which historian J. L. Heiberg calls "a mixture of brilliant intuition and childlike analogies",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      argues against the assertions from writers in late antiquity that Thales discovered and taught advanced concepts in these fields.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/John_Burnet_(classicist)" title="John Burnet (classicist)">
       John Burnet
      </a>
      (1892) noted
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <i>
        Lastly, we have one admitted instance of a philosophic guild, that of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagoreans" title="Pythagoreans">
         Pythagoreans
        </a>
        . And it will be found that the hypothesis, if it is to be called by that name, of a regular organisation of scientific activity will alone explain all the facts. The development of doctrine in the hands of
       </i>
       Thales
       <i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">
         Anaximander
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">
         Anaximenes
        </a>
        , for instance, can only be understood as the elaboration of a single idea in a school with a continuous tradition.
       </i>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretations">
       Interpretations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interpretations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In the long sojourn of philosophy, there has existed hardly a philosopher or historian of philosophy who did not mention Thales and try to characterize him in some way. He is generally recognized as having brought something new to human thought. Mathematics, astronomy and medicine already existed. Thales added something to these different collections of knowledge to produce a universality, which, as far as writing tells us, was not in tradition before, but resulted in a new field.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ever since, interested persons have been asking what that new something is. Answers fall into (at least) two categories, the theory and the method. Once an answer has been arrived at, the next logical step is to ask how Thales compares to other philosophers, which leads to his classification (rightly or wrongly).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">
       Theory
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Theory">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The most natural epithets of Thales are "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Materialist" title="Materialist">
       materialist
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">
       naturalist
      </a>
      ", which are based on ousia and physis. The
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">
       Catholic Encyclopedia
      </a>
      notes that Aristotle called him a physiologist, with the meaning "student of nature."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the other hand, he would have qualified as an early
      <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">
       physicist
      </a>
      , as did Aristotle. They studied corpora, "bodies", the medieval descendants of substances.
     </p>
     <p>
      Most agree that Thales' stamp on thought is the unity of substance, hence
      <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">
       Bertrand Russell
      </a>
      :
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "The view that all matter is one is quite a reputable scientific hypothesis."
      </dd>
      <dd>
       "...But it is still a handsome feat to have discovered that a substance remains the same in different
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">
        states of aggregation
       </a>
       ."
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Russell was only reflecting an established tradition; for example:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nietzsche" title="Nietzsche">
       Nietzsche
      </a>
      , in his
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_in_the_Tragic_Age_of_the_Greeks" title="Philosophy in the Tragic Age of the Greeks">
        Philosophy in the Tragic Age of the Greeks
       </a>
      </i>
      , wrote:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "Greek philosophy seems to begin with an absurd notion, with the proposition that
       <i>
        water
       </i>
       is the primal origin and the womb of all things. Is it really necessary for us to take serious notice of this proposition? It is, and for three reasons. First, because it tells us something about the primal origin of all things; second, because it does so in language devoid of image or fable, and finally, because contained in it, if only embryonically, is the thought, 'all things are one.'"
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      This sort of materialism, however, should not be confused with deterministic materialism. Thales was only trying to explain the unity observed in the free play of the qualities. The arrival of uncertainty in the modern world made possible a return to Thales; for example,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Elof_Boodin" title="John Elof Boodin">
       John Elof Boodin
      </a>
      writes ("God and Creation"):
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "We cannot read the universe from the past..."
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Boodin defines an "emergent" materialism, in which the objects of sense emerge uncertainly from the substrate. Thales is the innovator of this sort of materialism.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_theoretical_inquiry">
       Rise of theoretical inquiry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rise of theoretical inquiry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the West, Thales represents a new kind of inquiring community as well.
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Husserl" title="Edmund Husserl">
       Edmund Husserl
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      attempts to capture the new movement as follows. Philosophical man is a "new cultural configuration" based in stepping back from "pregiven tradition" and taking up a rational "inquiry into what is true in itself;" that is, an ideal of truth. It begins with isolated individuals such as Thales, but they are supported and cooperated with as time goes on. Finally the ideal transforms the norms of society, leaping across national borders.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Classification">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The term "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic" title="Pre-Socratic">
       Pre-Socratic
      </a>
      " derives ultimately from the philosopher Aristotle, who distinguished the early philosophers as concerning themselves with substance.
     </p>
     <p>
      Diogenes Laertius on the other hand took a strictly geographic and ethnic approach. Philosophers were either Ionian or Italian. He used "Ionian" in a broader sense, including also the Athenian academics, who were not Pre-Socratics. From a philosophic point of view, any grouping at all would have been just as effective. There is no basis for an Ionian or Italian unity. Some scholars, however, concede to Diogenes' scheme as far as referring to an "Ionian" school. There was no such school in any sense.
     </p>
     <p>
      The most popular approach refers to a Milesian school, which is more justifiable socially and philosophically. They sought for the substance of phenomena and may have studied with each other. Some ancient writers qualify them as Milesioi, "of Miletus."
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_others">
       Influence on others
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Influence on others">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louisstgaudens1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louisstgaudens1.jpg/220px-Louisstgaudens1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louisstgaudens1.jpg/330px-Louisstgaudens1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louisstgaudens1.jpg/440px-Louisstgaudens1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louisstgaudens1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Thales (Electricity)
        </i>
        , sculpture from "The Progress of Railroading" (1908), main facade of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_DC)" title="Union Station (Washington, DC)">
         Union Station (Washington, DC)
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thales had a profound influence on other Greek thinkers and therefore on
      <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
       Western
      </a>
      history. Some believe
      <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">
       Anaximander
      </a>
      was a pupil of Thales. Early sources report that one of Anaximander's more famous pupils,
      <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">
       Pythagoras
      </a>
      , visited Thales as a young man, and that Thales advised him to travel to Egypt to further his philosophical and mathematical studies.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many philosophers followed Thales' lead in searching for explanations in
      <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">
       nature
      </a>
      rather than in the supernatural; others returned to supernatural explanations, but couched them in the language of philosophy rather than of myth or of
      <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
       religion
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Looking specifically at Thales' influence during the pre-Socratic era, it is clear that he stood out as one of the first thinkers who thought more in the way of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">
        logos
       </a>
      </i>
      than
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">
        mythos
       </a>
      </i>
      . The difference between these two more profound ways of seeing the world is that
      <i>
       mythos
      </i>
      is concentrated around the stories of holy origin, while
      <i>
       logos
      </i>
      is concentrated around the argumentation. When the mythical man wants to explain the world the way he sees it, he explains it based on gods and powers. Mythical thought does not differentiate between things and persons
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      and furthermore it does not differentiate between nature and culture
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      . The way a
      <i>
       logos
      </i>
      thinker would present a world view is radically different from the way of the mythical thinker. In its concrete form,
      <i>
       logos
      </i>
      is a way of thinking not only about individualism
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2009)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      , but also the abstract
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2009)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      . Furthermore, it focuses on sensible and continuous argumentation. This lays the foundation of
      <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">
       philosophy
      </a>
      and its way of explaining the world in terms of abstract argumentation, and not in the way of gods and mythical stories
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reliability_of_sources">
       Reliability of sources
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reliability of sources">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="237" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png/180px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_59r_2.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thales,
        <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">
         Nuremberg Chronicle
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Because of Thales' elevated status in Greek culture an intense interest and admiration followed his reputation. Due to this following, the oral stories about his life were open to amplification and historical fabrication, even before they were written down generations later. Most modern dissension comes from trying to interpret what we know, in particular, distinguishing legend from fact.
     </p>
     <p>
      Historian D.R. Dicks and other historians divide the ancient sources about Thales into those before 320BC and those after that year (some such as
      <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">
       Proclus
      </a>
      writing in the 5th century C.E. and
      <a href="/wiki/Simplicius_of_Cilicia" title="Simplicius of Cilicia">
       Simplicius of Cilicia
      </a>
      in the 6th century C.E. writing nearly a millennium after his era).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first category includes
      <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
       Herodotus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">
       Plato
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">
       Aristophanes
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">
       Theophrastus
      </a>
      among others. The second category includes
      <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">
       Plautus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Aetius_(philosopher)" title="Aetius (philosopher)">
       Aetius
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">
       Eusebius
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
       Plutarch
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">
       Josephus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iamblichus" title="Iamblichus">
       Iamblichus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Lartius">
       Diogenes Lartius
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Theon_of_Smyrna" title="Theon of Smyrna">
       Theon of Smyrna
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">
       Apuleius
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">
       Clement of Alexandria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
       Pliny the Elder
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">
       John Tzetzes
      </a>
      among others.
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest sources on Thales (living before 320BC) are often the same for the other
      <a href="/wiki/Milesian_school" title="Milesian school">
       Milesian philosophers
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">
       Anaximander
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">
       Anaximenes
      </a>
      ). These sources were either roughly contemporaneous (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
       Herodotus
      </a>
      ) or lived within a few hundred years of his passing. Moreover, they were writing from an oral tradition that was widespread and well known in the Greece of their day.
     </p>
     <p>
      The latter sources on Thales are several "ascriptions of commentators and compilers who lived anything from 700 to 1,000years after his death"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which include "anecdotes of varying degrees of plausibility"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in the opinion of some historians (such as D. R. Dicks) of "no historical worth whatsoever".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks points out that there is no agreement "among the 'authorities' even on the most fundamental facts of his life--e.g. whether he was a Milesian or a Phoenician, whether he left any writings or not, whether he was married or single-much less on the actual ideas and achievements with which he is credited."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Contrasting the work of the more ancient writers with those of the later, Dicks points out that in the works of the early writers Thales and the other men who would be hailed as "the Seven Sages of Greece" had a different reputation than that which would be assigned to them by later authors. Closer to their own era, Thales,
      <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">
       Solon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bias_of_Priene" title="Bias of Priene">
       Bias of Priene
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pittacus_of_Mytilene" title="Pittacus of Mytilene">
       Pittacus of Mytilene
      </a>
      and others were hailed as "essentially practical men who played leading roles in the affairs of their respective states, and were far better known to the earlier Greeks as lawgivers and statesmen than as profound thinkers and philosophers."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, Plato praises him (coupled with
      <a href="/wiki/Anacharsis" title="Anacharsis">
       Anacharsis
      </a>
      ) for being the originator of the potter's wheel and the anchor.
     </p>
     <p>
      Only in the writings of the second group of writers (working after 320BC) do "we obtain the picture of Thales as the pioneer in Greek scientific thinking, particularly in regard to mathematics and astronomy which he is supposed to have learnt about in Babylonia and Egypt."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rather than "the earlier tradition [where] he is a favourite example of the intelligent man who possesses some technical 'know how'...the later doxographers [such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">
       Dicaearchus
      </a>
      in the latter half of the fourth century BC] foist on to him any number of discoveries and achievements, in order to build him up as a figure of superhuman wisdom."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dicks points out a further problem arises in the surviving information on Thales, for rather than using ancient sources closer to the era of Thales, the authors in later antiquity ("epitomators, excerptors, and compilers"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) actually "preferred to use one or more intermediaries, so that what we actually read in them comes to us not even at second, but at third or fourth or fifth hand. ...Obviously this use of intermediate sources, copied and recopied from century to century, with each writer adding additional pieces of information of greater or less plausibility from his own knowledge, provided a fertile field for errors in transmission, wrong ascriptions, and fictitious attributions".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dicks points out that "certain doctrines that later commentators invented for Thales...were then accepted into the biographical tradition" being copied by subsequent writers who were then cited by those coming after them "and thus, because they may be repeated by different authors relying on different sources, may produce an illusory impression of genuineness."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Doubts even exist when considering the philosophical positions held to originate in Thales "in reality these stem directly from Aristotle's own interpretations which then became incorporated in the doxographical tradition as erroneous ascriptions to Thales".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (The same treatment was given by Aristotle to
      <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">
       Anaxagoras
      </a>
      .)
     </p>
     <p>
      Most philosophic analyses of the philosophy of Thales come from
      <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
       Aristotle
      </a>
      , a professional philosopher, tutor of
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
       Alexander the Great
      </a>
      , who wrote 200years after Thales' death. Aristotle, judging from his surviving books, does not seem to have access to any works by Thales, although he probably had access to works of other authors about Thales, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
       Herodotus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">
       Hecataeus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">
       Plato
      </a>
      etc., as well as others whose work is now extinct. It was Aristotle's express goal to present Thales' work not because it was significant in itself, but as a prelude to his own work in natural philosophy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Kirk" title="Geoffrey Kirk">
       Geoffrey Kirk
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Raven" title="John Raven">
       John Raven
      </a>
      , English compilers of the fragments of the Pre-Socratics, assert that Aristotle's "judgments are often distorted by his view of earlier philosophy as a stumbling progress toward the truth that Aristotle himself revealed in his physical doctrines."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There was also an extensive oral tradition. Both the oral and the written were commonly read or known by all educated men in the region.
     </p>
     <p>
      Aristotle's philosophy had a distinct stamp: it professed the theory of matter and form, which modern scholastics have dubbed
      <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">
       hylomorphism
      </a>
      . Though once very widespread, it was not generally adopted by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rationalist" title="Rationalist">
       rationalist
      </a>
      and modern science, as it mainly is useful in
      <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
       metaphysical
      </a>
      analyses, but does not lend itself to the detail that is of interest to modern science. It is not clear that the theory of matter and form existed as early as Thales, and if it did, whether Thales espoused it.
     </p>
     <p>
      While some historians, like B. Snell, maintain that Aristotle was relying on a pre-Platonic written record by
      <a href="/wiki/Hippias" title="Hippias">
       Hippias
      </a>
      rather than oral tradition, this is a controversial position. Representing the scholarly consensus Dicks states that "the tradition about him even as early as the fifth century B.C., was evidently based entirely on hearsay....It would seem that already by Aristotle's time the early Ionians were largely names only to which popular tradition attached various ideas or achievements with greater or less plausibility".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicks_10-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dicks-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He points out that works confirmed to have existed in the sixth century BC by Anaximander and Xenophanes had already disappeared by the fourth century BC, so the chances of Pre-Socratic material surviving to the age of Aristotle is almost nil (even less likely for Aristotle's pupils Theophrastus and Eudemus and less likely still for those following after them).
     </p>
     <p>
      The main secondary source concerning the details of Thales' life and career is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_Laertius" title="Diogenes Laertius">
       Diogenes Laertius
      </a>
      , "
      <i>
       Lives of Eminent Philosophers
      </i>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is primarily a biographical work, as the name indicates. Compared to Aristotle, Diogenes is not much of a philosopher. He is the one who, in the Prologue to that work, is responsible for the division of the early philosophers into "Ionian" and "Italian", but he places the Academics in the Ionian school and otherwise evidences considerable disarray and contradiction, especially in the long section on forerunners of the "Ionian School". Diogenes quotes two letters attributed to Thales, but Diogenes wrote some eight centuries after Thales' death and that his sources often contained "unreliable or even fabricated information",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      hence the concern for separating fact from legend in accounts of Thales.
     </p>
     <p>
      It is due to this use of hearsay and a lack of citing original sources that leads some historians, like Dicks and Werner Jaeger, to look at the late origin of the traditional picture of Pre-Socratic philosophy and view the whole idea as a construct from a later age, "the whole picture that has come down to us of the history of early philosophy was fashioned during the two or three generations from Plato to the immediate pupils of Aristotle".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

          <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0258%3Abook%3D1%3Achapter%3D1" rel="nofollow">

         <a class="external text" href="http://www.bartleby.com/61/77/P0277700.html" rel="nofollow">
          physics
         </a>
         comes from it, but the latter is a Greek loan. In addition the quite ancient native English word
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.bartleby.com/61/roots/IE62.html" rel="nofollow">
          be
         </a>
         comes from the same
         <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
          Indo-European
         </a>
         root.
